<tr style="background:#f8eaba;">
<td style="width:40%; max-height:1px; border-top:2px solid transparent; border-right:1px solid transparent;">
</td>
<td style="width:60%; max-height:1px; border-top:2px solid transparent; border-left:1px solid transparent;">

<p><b>Carbon dioxide</b> (<a href="/wiki/Chemical_formula" title="Chemical formula">chemical formula</a> <b>CO<sub>2</sub></b>) is a colorless <a href="/wiki/Gas" title="Gas">gas</a> with a density about 60% higher than that of dry air.  Carbon dioxide consists of a <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atom</a> <a href="/wiki/Covalent_bond" title="Covalent bond">covalently</a> <a href="/wiki/Double_bond" title="Double bond">double bonded</a> to two <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> atoms. It occurs naturally in <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Earth" title="Atmosphere of Earth">Earth's atmosphere</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Trace_gas" title="Trace gas">trace gas</a>. The current concentration is about 0.04% (410 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parts_per_million" title="Parts per million">ppm</a>) by volume, having risen from pre-industrial levels of 280 ppm.  Natural sources include <a href="/wiki/Volcano" title="Volcano">volcanoes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hot_spring" title="Hot spring">hot springs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geyser" title="Geyser">geysers</a>, and it is freed from <a href="/wiki/Carbonate_rock" title="Carbonate rock">carbonate rocks</a> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dissolution_(chemistry)" title="Dissolution (chemistry)">dissolution</a> in water and acids. Because carbon dioxide is soluble in water, it occurs naturally in <a href="/wiki/Groundwater" title="Groundwater">groundwater</a>, <a href="/wiki/River" title="River">rivers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lake" title="Lake">lakes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ice_cap" title="Ice cap">ice caps</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glacier" title="Glacier">glaciers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Seawater" title="Seawater">seawater</a>. It is present in deposits of <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">petroleum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a>. Carbon dioxide is odorless at normally encountered concentrations. However, at high concentrations, it has a sharp and acidic odor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AirProductsMSDS_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-AirProductsMSDS-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>As the source of available carbon in the <a href="/wiki/Carbon_cycle" title="Carbon cycle">carbon cycle</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atmospheric_carbon_dioxide" title="Atmospheric carbon dioxide">atmospheric carbon dioxide</a> is the primary carbon source for <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life on Earth</a> and its concentration in Earth's pre-industrial atmosphere since late in the <a href="/wiki/Precambrian" title="Precambrian">Precambrian</a> has been regulated by <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthetic</a> organisms and geological phenomena. <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">Plants</a>, <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cyanobacteria" title="Cyanobacteria">cyanobacteria</a> use <a href="/wiki/Light" title="Light">light</a> <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> to <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesize</a> <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrate</a> from carbon dioxide and water, with oxygen produced as a waste product.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>CO<sub>2</sub> is produced by all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aerobic_organisms" title="Aerobic organisms">aerobic organisms</a> when they metabolize carbohydrates and lipids to produce energy by <a href="/wiki/Cellular_respiration" title="Cellular respiration">respiration</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> It is returned to water via the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fish_respiration" title="Fish respiration">gills of fish</a> and to the air via the lungs of air-breathing land animals, including humans. Carbon dioxide is produced during the processes of <a href="/wiki/Decomposition" title="Decomposition">decay</a> of organic materials and the <a href="/wiki/Fermentation" title="Fermentation">fermentation</a> of sugars in <a href="/wiki/Bread" title="Bread">bread</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beer" title="Beer">beer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wine" title="Wine">wine</a> making. It is produced by combustion of <a href="/wiki/Wood" title="Wood">wood</a> and other organic materials and <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuels</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peat" title="Peat">peat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">petroleum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a>. It is an unwanted byproduct in many large scale <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxidation" title="Oxidation">oxidation</a> processes, for example, in the production of <a href="/wiki/Acrylic_acid" title="Acrylic acid">acrylic acid</a> (over 5 million tons/year).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>It is a versatile industrial material, used, for example, as an inert gas in welding and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fire_extinguishers" title="Fire extinguishers">fire extinguishers</a>, as a pressurizing gas in air guns and oil recovery, as a chemical feedstock and as a supercritical fluid solvent in decaffeination of coffee<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Supercritical_drying" title="Supercritical drying">supercritical drying</a>. It is added to drinking water and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbonated_beverage" title="Carbonated beverage">carbonated beverages</a> including <a href="/wiki/Beer" title="Beer">beer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sparkling_wine" title="Sparkling wine">sparkling wine</a> to add <a href="/wiki/Effervescence" title="Effervescence">effervescence</a>. The frozen solid form of CO<sub>2</sub>, known as <i><a href="/wiki/Dry_ice" title="Dry ice">dry ice</a></i> is used as a refrigerant and as an abrasive in <a href="/wiki/Dry-ice_blasting" title="Dry-ice blasting">dry-ice blasting</a>.
</p><p>Carbon dioxide is the most significant long-lived <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a> in <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_in_Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Carbon dioxide in Earth's atmosphere">Earth's atmosphere</a>. Since the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a> anthropogenic emissions – primarily from use of fossil fuels and <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a> – have rapidly increased its concentration in the atmosphere, leading to <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a>.  Carbon dioxide also causes <a href="/wiki/Ocean_acidification" title="Ocean acidification">ocean acidification</a> because it dissolves in water to form <a href="/wiki/Carbonic_acid" title="Carbonic acid">carbonic acid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NRC2010_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-NRC2010-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carbon-dioxide-crystal-3D-vdW.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1066" data-file-width="1100" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Carbon-dioxide-crystal-3D-vdW.png/170px-Carbon-dioxide-crystal-3D-vdW.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Carbon-dioxide-crystal-3D-vdW.png/255px-Carbon-dioxide-crystal-3D-vdW.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Carbon-dioxide-crystal-3D-vdW.png/340px-Carbon-dioxide-crystal-3D-vdW.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Carbon-dioxide-crystal-3D-vdW.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Crystal structure of <a href="/wiki/Dry_ice" title="Dry ice">dry ice</a></div></div></div>
<p>Carbon dioxide was the first gas to be described as a discrete substance. In about 1640,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Flemish_people" title="Flemish people">Flemish</a> chemist <a href="/wiki/Jan_Baptist_van_Helmont" title="Jan Baptist van Helmont">Jan Baptist van Helmont</a> observed that when he burned <a href="/wiki/Charcoal" title="Charcoal">charcoal</a> in a closed vessel, the mass of the resulting <a href="/wiki/Ash_(analytical_chemistry)" title="Ash (analytical chemistry)">ash</a> was much less than that of the original charcoal. His interpretation was that the rest of the charcoal had been transmuted into an invisible substance he termed a "gas" or "wild spirit" (<i>spiritus sylvestris</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>The properties of carbon dioxide were further studied in the 1750s by the <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scottish</a> physician <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Black" title="Joseph Black">Joseph Black</a>. He found that <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a> (<a href="/wiki/Calcium_carbonate" title="Calcium carbonate">calcium carbonate</a>) could be heated or treated with <a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">acids</a> to yield a gas he called "fixed air." He observed that the fixed air was denser than air and supported neither flame nor animal life. Black also found that when bubbled through <a href="/wiki/Limewater" title="Limewater">limewater</a> (a saturated aqueous solution of <a href="/wiki/Calcium_hydroxide" title="Calcium hydroxide">calcium hydroxide</a>), it would <a href="/wiki/Precipitation_(chemistry)" title="Precipitation (chemistry)">precipitate</a> calcium carbonate. He used this phenomenon to illustrate that carbon dioxide is produced by animal respiration and microbial fermentation. In 1772, English chemist <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Priestley" title="Joseph Priestley">Joseph Priestley</a> published a paper entitled <i>Impregnating Water with Fixed Air</i> in which he described a process of dripping <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a> (or <i>oil of vitriol</i> as Priestley knew it) on chalk in order to produce carbon dioxide, and forcing the gas to dissolve by agitating a bowl of water in contact with the gas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Priestley_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Priestley-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>Carbon dioxide was first liquefied (at elevated pressures) in 1823 by <a href="/wiki/Humphry_Davy" title="Humphry Davy">Humphry Davy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Michael_Faraday" title="Michael Faraday">Michael Faraday</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davy_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davy-17">[17]</a></sup> The earliest description of solid carbon dioxide was given by <a href="/wiki/Adrien-Jean-Pierre_Thilorier" title="Adrien-Jean-Pierre Thilorier">Adrien-Jean-Pierre Thilorier</a>, who in 1835 opened a pressurized container of liquid carbon dioxide, only to find that the cooling produced by the rapid evaporation of the liquid yielded a "snow" of solid CO<sub>2</sub>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_and_physical_properties">Chemical and physical properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Chemical and physical properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Co2_vibrations.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="319" data-file-width="886" height="79" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Co2_vibrations.svg/220px-Co2_vibrations.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Co2_vibrations.svg/330px-Co2_vibrations.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Co2_vibrations.svg/440px-Co2_vibrations.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Co2_vibrations.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Infrared_spectroscopy#Number_of_vibrational_modes" title="Infrared spectroscopy">Stretching and bending oscillations</a> of the CO<sub>2</sub> carbon dioxide molecule. Upper left: symmetric stretching. Upper right: antisymmetric stretching. Lower line: degenerate pair of bending modes.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure_and_bonding">Structure and bonding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Structure and bonding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Molecular_orbital_diagram#Carbon_dioxide" title="Molecular orbital diagram">Molecular orbital diagram § Carbon dioxide</a></div>
<p>The carbon dioxide molecule is linear and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Centrosymmetric" title="Centrosymmetric">centrosymmetric</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Carbon%E2%80%93oxygen_bond" title="Carbon–oxygen bond">carbon–oxygen bond</a> length is 116.3 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Picometer" title="Picometer">pm</a>, noticeably shorter than the <a href="/wiki/Bond_length" title="Bond length">bond length</a> of a C–O single bond and even shorter than most other C–O multiply-bonded functional groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Green_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Green-20">[20]</a></sup>  Since it is centrosymmetric, the molecule has no electrical <a href="/wiki/Dipole" title="Dipole">dipole</a>. Consequently, only two vibrational bands are observed in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IR_spectrum" title="IR spectrum">IR spectrum</a> – an antisymmetric stretching mode at 2349 cm<sup>−1</sup> and a <a href="/wiki/Degenerate_energy_levels" title="Degenerate energy levels">degenerate</a> pair of bending modes at 667 cm<sup>−1</sup>. There is also a symmetric stretching mode at 1388 cm<sup>−1</sup> which is only observed in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Raman_spectrum" title="Raman spectrum">Raman spectrum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_aqueous_solution">In aqueous solution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: In aqueous solution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Carbonic_acid" title="Carbonic acid">Carbonic acid</a></div>
<p>Carbon dioxide is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soluble" title="Soluble">soluble</a> in water, in which it reversibly forms <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> (<a href="/wiki/Carbonic_acid" title="Carbonic acid">carbonic acid</a>), which is a <a href="/wiki/Acid_strength" title="Acid strength">weak acid</a> since its ionization in water is incomplete.
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> + <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> ⇌ <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span></dd></dl>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Henry%27s_law" title="Henry's law">hydration equilibrium constant</a> of carbonic acid is <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle K_{\mathrm {h} }={\frac {\rm {[H_{2}CO_{3}]}}{\rm {[CO_{2}(aq)]}}}=1.70\times 10^{-3}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>K</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="normal">h</mi>
</mrow>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo stretchy="false">[</mo>

<mi mathvariant="normal">H</mi>

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle K_{\mathrm {h} }={\frac {\rm {[H_{2}CO_{3}]}}{\rm {[CO_{2}(aq)]}}}=1.70\times 10^{-3}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="K_{\mathrm {h} }={\frac {\rm {[H_{2}CO_{3}]}}{\rm {[CO_{2}(aq)]}}}=1.70\times 10^{-3}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/880be9280cefa51b14dc80f037e4296c81a15e6b" style="vertical-align: -2.671ex; width:31.834ex; height:6.509ex;"/></span> (at 25 °C). Hence, the majority of the carbon dioxide is not converted into carbonic acid, but remains as CO<sub>2</sub> molecules, not affecting the pH.
</p><p>The relative concentrations of <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>, H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>, and the <a href="/wiki/Deprotonation" title="Deprotonation">deprotonated</a> forms <span class="chemf nowrap">HCO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> (<a href="/wiki/Bicarbonate" title="Bicarbonate">bicarbonate</a>) and <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>(<a href="/wiki/Carbonate" title="Carbonate">carbonate</a>) depend on the <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a>. As shown in a <a href="/wiki/Bjerrum_plot" title="Bjerrum plot">Bjerrum plot</a>, in neutral or slightly alkaline water (pH &gt; 6.5), the bicarbonate form predominates (&gt;50%) becoming the most prevalent (&gt;95%) at the pH of seawater. In very alkaline water (pH &gt; 10.4), the predominant (&gt;50%) form is carbonate. The oceans, being mildly alkaline with typical pH = 8.2–8.5, contain about 120 mg of bicarbonate per liter.
</p><p>Being <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diprotic_acid" title="Diprotic acid">diprotic</a>, carbonic acid has two <a href="/wiki/Acid_dissociation_constant" title="Acid dissociation constant">acid dissociation constants</a>, the first one for the dissociation into the bicarbonate (also called hydrogen carbonate) ion (HCO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup>):
</p>
<dl><dd>H<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> ⇌ HCO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup> + H<sup>+</sup></dd>
<dd><i>K</i><sub>a1</sub> = <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6999250000000000000♠</span>2.5<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−4</sup> mol/L</span>; p<i>K</i><sub>a1</sub> = 3.6 at 25 °C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Green_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-Green-20">[20]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<p>This is the <i>true</i> first acid dissociation constant, defined as <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle K_{a1}={\frac {\rm {[HCO_{3}^{-}][H^{+}]}}{\rm {[H_{2}CO_{3}]}}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>

The much smaller and often-quoted value near <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6993416000000000000♠</span>4.16<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−7</sup></span> is an <i>apparent</i> value calculated on the (incorrect) assumption that all dissolved CO<sub>2</sub> is present as carbonic acid, so that <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle K_{\mathrm {a1} }{\rm {(apparent)}}={\frac {\rm {[HCO_{3}^{-}][H^{+}]}}{\rm {[H_{2}CO_{3}]+[CO_{2}(aq)]}}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</math></span><img alt="K_{\mathrm {a1} }{\rm {(apparent)}}={\frac {\rm {[HCO_{3}^{-}][H^{+}]}}{\rm {[H_{2}CO_{3}]+[CO_{2}(aq)]}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d4cac9482fd21cb58444f2621f245d7b1d835485" style="vertical-align: -2.671ex; width:40.166ex; height:6.843ex;"/></span>. Since most of the dissolved CO<sub>2</sub> remains as CO<sub>2</sub> molecules, <i>K</i><sub>a1</sub>(apparent) has a much larger denominator and a much smaller value than the true <i>K</i><sub>a1</sub>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Bicarbonate" title="Bicarbonate">bicarbonate</a> ion is an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amphoteric" title="Amphoteric">amphoteric</a> species that can act as an acid or as a base, depending on pH of the solution. At high <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a>, it dissociates significantly into the <a href="/wiki/Carbonate" title="Carbonate">carbonate</a> ion (CO<sub>3</sub><sup>2−</sup>):
</p>
<dl><dd>HCO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup> ⇌ CO<sub>3</sub><sup>2−</sup> + H<sup>+</sup></dd>
<dd><i>K</i><sub>a2</sub> = <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6992469000000000000♠</span>4.69<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−11</sup> mol/L</span>; p<i>K</i><sub>a2</sub> = 10.329</dd></dl>
<p>In organisms carbonic acid production is catalysed by the <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzyme</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbonic_anhydrase" title="Carbonic anhydrase">carbonic anhydrase</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_reactions_of_CO2">Chemical reactions of CO<sub>2</sub></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Chemical reactions of CO2">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg"><img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs expansion</b>. <small>You can help by <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.</small> <small><i>(June 2014)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>CO<sub>2</sub> is a weak <a href="/wiki/Electrophile" title="Electrophile">electrophile</a>. Its reaction with basic water illustrates this property, in which case <a href="/wiki/Hydroxide" title="Hydroxide">hydroxide</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Nucleophile" title="Nucleophile">nucleophile</a>. Other nucleophiles react as well. For example, <a href="/wiki/Carbanion" title="Carbanion">carbanions</a> as provided by  <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grignard_reagent" title="Grignard reagent">Grignard reagents</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organolithium_compound" title="Organolithium compound">organolithium compounds</a> react with CO<sub>2</sub> to give <a href="/wiki/Carboxylate" title="Carboxylate">carboxylates</a>:
</p>
<dl><dd>MR  +  CO<sub>2</sub>   →   RCO<sub>2</sub>M</dd>
<dd>where M = <a href="/wiki/Lithium" title="Lithium">Li</a> or <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">Mg</a><a href="/wiki/Bromine" title="Bromine">Br</a> and R = <a href="/wiki/Alkyl" title="Alkyl">alkyl</a> or <a href="/wiki/Aryl" title="Aryl">aryl</a>.</dd></dl>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Metal_carbon_dioxide_complex" title="Metal carbon dioxide complex">metal carbon dioxide complexes</a>, CO<sub>2</sub> serves as a <a href="/wiki/Ligand" title="Ligand">ligand</a>, which can facilitate the conversion of CO<sub>2</sub> to other chemicals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>The reduction of CO<sub>2</sub> to <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">CO</a> is ordinarily a difficult and slow reaction:
</p>
<dl><dd>CO<sub>2</sub> +  2 e<sup>−</sup>  +  2H<sup>+</sup>   →   CO  +  H<sub>2</sub>O</dd></dl>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photoautotrophs" title="Photoautotrophs">Photoautotrophs</a> (i.e. plants and <a href="/wiki/Cyanobacteria" title="Cyanobacteria">cyanobacteria</a>) use the energy contained in sunlight to <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesize</a> simple sugars from CO<sub>2</sub> absorbed from the air and water:
</p>
<dl><dd><i>n</i> CO<sub>2</sub> + <i>n</i> <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> → <span class="chemf nowrap">(CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"><i>n</i></sub></span></span> + <i>n</i> <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span></dd></dl>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Redox_potential" title="Redox potential">redox potential</a> for this reaction near pH 7 is about −0.53 V <i>versus</i> the <a href="/wiki/Standard_hydrogen_electrode" title="Standard hydrogen electrode">standard hydrogen electrode</a>. The nickel-containing enzyme <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide_dehydrogenase" title="Carbon monoxide dehydrogenase">carbon monoxide dehydrogenase</a> catalyses this process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_properties">Physical properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Physical properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_data" title="Carbon dioxide data">Carbon dioxide data</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dry_Ice_Pellets_Subliming.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="523" data-file-width="609" height="189" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Dry_Ice_Pellets_Subliming.jpg/220px-Dry_Ice_Pellets_Subliming.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Dry_Ice_Pellets_Subliming.jpg/330px-Dry_Ice_Pellets_Subliming.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Dry_Ice_Pellets_Subliming.jpg/440px-Dry_Ice_Pellets_Subliming.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dry_Ice_Pellets_Subliming.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pellets of "dry ice", a common form of solid carbon dioxide</div></div></div>
<p>Carbon dioxide is colorless. At low concentrations the gas is odorless; however, at sufficiently-high concentrations, it has a sharp, acidic odor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AirProductsMSDS_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-AirProductsMSDS-1">[1]</a></sup> At <a href="/wiki/Standard_conditions_for_temperature_and_pressure" title="Standard conditions for temperature and pressure">standard temperature and pressure</a>, the density of carbon dioxide is around 1.98 kg/m<sup>3</sup>, about 1.67 times that of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Earth's atmosphere">air</a>.
</p><p>Carbon dioxide has no liquid state at pressures below 5.1 <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_(unit)" title="Atmosphere (unit)">standard atmospheres</a> (520 kPa). At 1 atmosphere (near mean sea level pressure), the gas <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deposition_(physics)" title="Deposition (physics)">deposits</a> directly to a solid at temperatures below −78.5 °C (−109.3 °F; 194.7 K) and the solid <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sublimation_(chemistry)" title="Sublimation (chemistry)">sublimes</a> directly to a gas above −78.5 °C. In its solid state, carbon dioxide is commonly called <a href="/wiki/Dry_ice" title="Dry ice">dry ice</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carbon_dioxide_pressure-temperature_phase_diagram.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="700" data-file-width="742" height="208" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Carbon_dioxide_pressure-temperature_phase_diagram.svg/220px-Carbon_dioxide_pressure-temperature_phase_diagram.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Carbon_dioxide_pressure-temperature_phase_diagram.svg/330px-Carbon_dioxide_pressure-temperature_phase_diagram.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Carbon_dioxide_pressure-temperature_phase_diagram.svg/440px-Carbon_dioxide_pressure-temperature_phase_diagram.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Carbon_dioxide_pressure-temperature_phase_diagram.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pressure–temperature <a href="/wiki/Phase_diagram" title="Phase diagram">phase diagram</a> of carbon dioxide</div></div></div>
<p>Liquid carbon dioxide forms only at <a href="/wiki/Pressure" title="Pressure">pressures</a> above 5.1 atm; the <a href="/wiki/Triple_point" title="Triple point">triple point</a> of carbon dioxide is about 5.1 <a href="/wiki/Bar_(unit)" title="Bar (unit)">bar</a> (517 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/KPa" title="KPa">kPa</a>) at 217 K (see phase diagram). The <a href="/wiki/Critical_point_(thermodynamics)" title="Critical point (thermodynamics)">critical point</a> is 7.38 MPa at 31.1 °C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> Another form of solid carbon dioxide observed at high pressure is an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amorphous" title="Amorphous">amorphous</a> glass-like solid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> This form of glass, called <i><a href="/wiki/Amorphous_carbonia" title="Amorphous carbonia">carbonia</a></i>, is produced by <a href="/wiki/Supercooling" title="Supercooling">supercooling</a> heated CO<sub>2</sub> at extreme pressure (40–48 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GPa" title="GPa">GPa</a> or about 400,000 atmospheres) in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diamond_anvil" title="Diamond anvil">diamond anvil</a>. This discovery confirmed the theory that carbon dioxide could exist in a glass state similar to other members of its elemental family, like <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silica" title="Silica">silica glass</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Germanium_dioxide" title="Germanium dioxide">germanium dioxide</a>. Unlike silica and germania glasses, however, carbonia glass is not stable at normal pressures and reverts to gas when pressure is released.
</p><p>At temperatures and pressures above the critical point, carbon dioxide behaves as a <a href="/wiki/Supercritical_fluid" title="Supercritical fluid">supercritical fluid</a> known as <a href="/wiki/Supercritical_carbon_dioxide" title="Supercritical carbon dioxide">supercritical carbon dioxide</a>.  In this state it is starting (as of 2018) to be used for power generation. <a href="/wiki/Supercritical_carbon_dioxide#Power_generation" title="Supercritical carbon dioxide">Supercritical_carbon_dioxide#Power_generation</a>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Isolation_and_production">Isolation and production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Isolation and production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Carbon dioxide can be obtained by <a href="/wiki/Distillation" title="Distillation">distillation</a> from air, but the method is inefficient. Industrially, carbon dioxide is predominantly an unrecovered waste product, produced by several methods which may be practiced at various scales.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kirk_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-kirk-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Combustion" title="Combustion">combustion</a> of all <a href="/wiki/Carbon-based_fuel" title="Carbon-based fuel">carbon-based fuels</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> (<a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a>), petroleum distillates (<a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">gasoline</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diesel_fuel" title="Diesel fuel">diesel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kerosene" title="Kerosene">kerosene</a>, <a href="/wiki/Propane" title="Propane">propane</a>), coal, wood and generic organic matter produces carbon dioxide and, except in the case of pure carbon, water. As an example, the chemical reaction between methane and oxygen:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="chemf nowrap">CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>+ 2 O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>→ CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>+ 2 H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span></dd></dl>
<p>It is produced by thermal decomposition of limestone, <span class="chemf nowrap">CaCO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> by heating (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calcining" title="Calcining">calcining</a>) at about 850 °C (1,560 °F), in the manufacture of <a href="/wiki/Calcium_oxide" title="Calcium oxide">quicklime</a> (<a href="/wiki/Calcium_oxide" title="Calcium oxide">calcium oxide</a>, <span class="chemf nowrap">CaO</span>), a compound that has many industrial uses:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="chemf nowrap">CaCO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>→ CaO + CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span></dd></dl>
<p><a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">Iron</a> is reduced from its oxides with <a href="/wiki/Coke_(fuel)" title="Coke (fuel)">coke</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Blast_furnace" title="Blast furnace">blast furnace</a>, producing <a href="/wiki/Pig_iron" title="Pig iron">pig iron</a> and carbon dioxide:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>Carbon dioxide is a byproduct of the industrial production of hydrogen by <a href="/wiki/Steam_reforming" title="Steam reforming">steam reforming</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_gas_shift_reaction" title="Water gas shift reaction">water gas shift reaction</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ammonia_production" title="Ammonia production">ammonia production</a>. These processes begin with the reaction of water and natural gas (mainly methane).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> This is a major source of food-grade carbon dioxide for use in carbonation of <a href="/wiki/Beer" title="Beer">beer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Soft_drink" title="Soft drink">soft drinks</a>, and is also used for stunning animals such as <a href="/wiki/Poultry" title="Poultry">poultry</a>. In the summer of 2018 a shortage of carbon dioxide for these purposes arose in Europe due to the temporary shut-down of several ammonia plants for maintenance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>Acids liberate CO<sub>2</sub> from most metal carbonates. Consequently, it may be obtained directly from natural carbon dioxide <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spring_(hydrosphere)" title="Spring (hydrosphere)">springs</a>, where it is produced by the action of acidified water on <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a> or <a href="/wiki/Dolomite" title="Dolomite">dolomite</a>. The reaction between <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">hydrochloric acid</a> and calcium carbonate (limestone or chalk) is shown below:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="chemf nowrap">CaCO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>+ 2 HCl → CaCl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>+ H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span></dd></dl>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Carbonic_acid" title="Carbonic acid">carbonic acid</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>) then decomposes to water and CO<sub>2</sub>:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>→ CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>+ H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span></dd></dl>
<p>Such reactions are accompanied by foaming or bubbling, or both, as the gas is released. They have widespread uses in industry because they can be used to neutralize waste acid streams.
</p><p>Carbon dioxide is a by-product of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fermentation_(biochemistry)" title="Fermentation (biochemistry)">fermentation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Sugar" title="Sugar">sugar</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Brewing" title="Brewing">brewing</a> of <a href="/wiki/Beer" title="Beer">beer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Whisky" title="Whisky">whisky</a> and other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alcoholic_beverage" title="Alcoholic beverage">alcoholic beverages</a> and in the production of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bioethanol" title="Bioethanol">bioethanol</a>.
<a href="/wiki/Yeast" title="Yeast">Yeast</a> metabolizes <a href="/wiki/Sugar" title="Sugar">sugar</a> to produce CO<sub>2</sub> and <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a>, also known as alcohol, as follows:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="chemf nowrap">C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">12</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span> → <span class="chemf nowrap">2 CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>+ 2 C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span>OH</span></dd></dl>
<p>All <a href="/wiki/Cellular_respiration" title="Cellular respiration">aerobic</a> organisms produce CO<sub>2</sub> when they oxidize <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fatty_acid" title="Fatty acid">fatty acids</a>, and proteins. The large number of reactions involved are exceedingly complex and not described easily. Refer to (<a href="/wiki/Cellular_respiration" title="Cellular respiration">cellular respiration</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anaerobic_respiration" title="Anaerobic respiration">anaerobic respiration</a> and <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a>). The equation for the respiration of glucose and other <a href="/wiki/Monosaccharide" title="Monosaccharide">monosaccharides</a> is:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="chemf nowrap">C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">12</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span> + <span class="chemf nowrap">6 O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> → <span class="chemf nowrap">6 CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> + <span class="chemf nowrap">6 H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span></dd></dl>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anaerobic_organisms" title="Anaerobic organisms">Anaerobic organisms</a> decompose organic material producing methane and carbon dioxide together with traces of other compounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> Regardless of the type of organic material, the production of gases follows well defined <a href="/wiki/Chemical_kinetics" title="Chemical kinetics">kinetic pattern</a>. Carbon dioxide comprises about 40–45% of the gas that emanates from decomposition in landfills (termed "<a href="/wiki/Landfill_gas" title="Landfill gas">landfill gas</a>"). Most of the remaining 50–55% is methane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Carbon dioxide is used by the food industry, the oil industry, and the chemical industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kirk_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-kirk-28">[28]</a></sup>
The compound has varied commercial uses but one of its greatest use as a chemical is in the production of carbonated beverages; it provides the sparkle in carbonated beverages such as soda water, beer and sparkling wine.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Precursor_to_chemicals">Precursor to chemicals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Precursor to chemicals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg"><img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs expansion</b>. <small>You can help by <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.</small> <small><i>(July 2014)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>In the chemical industry, carbon dioxide is mainly consumed as an ingredient in the production of <a href="/wiki/Urea" title="Urea">urea</a>, with a smaller fraction being used to produce <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">methanol</a> and a range of other products,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> such as metal <a href="/wiki/Carbonate" title="Carbonate">carbonates</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bicarbonate" title="Bicarbonate">bicarbonates</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Some carboxylic acid derivatives such as <a href="/wiki/Sodium_salicylate" title="Sodium salicylate">sodium salicylate</a> are prepared using CO<sub>2</sub> by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kolbe-Schmitt_reaction" title="Kolbe-Schmitt reaction">Kolbe-Schmitt reaction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition to conventional processes using CO<sub>2</sub> for chemical production, electrochemical methods are also being explored at a research level. In particular, the use of renewable energy for production of fuels from CO<sub>2</sub> (such as methanol) is attractive as this could result in fuels that could be easily transported and used within conventional combustion technologies but have no net CO<sub>2</sub> emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foods">Foods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Foods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Soda_bubbles_macro.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="750" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Soda_bubbles_macro.jpg/220px-Soda_bubbles_macro.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Soda_bubbles_macro.jpg/330px-Soda_bubbles_macro.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Soda_bubbles_macro.jpg/440px-Soda_bubbles_macro.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Soda_bubbles_macro.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Carbon dioxide bubbles in a soft drink.</div></div></div>
<p>Carbon dioxide is a <a href="/wiki/Food_additive" title="Food additive">food additive</a> used as a propellant and acidity regulator in the food industry. It is approved for usage in the EU<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> (listed as <a href="/wiki/E_number" title="E number">E number</a> E290), US<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> and Australia and New Zealand<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> (listed by its INS number 290).
</p><p>A candy called <a href="/wiki/Pop_Rocks" title="Pop Rocks">Pop Rocks</a> is pressurized with carbon dioxide gas<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> at about 4 x 10<sup>6</sup> Pa (40 bar, 580 psi). When placed in the mouth, it dissolves (just like other hard candy) and releases the gas bubbles with an audible pop.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Leavening_agent" title="Leavening agent">Leavening agents</a> cause dough to rise by producing carbon dioxide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Baker%27s_yeast" title="Baker's yeast">Baker's yeast</a> produces carbon dioxide by fermentation of sugars within the dough, while chemical leaveners such as <a href="/wiki/Baking_powder" title="Baking powder">baking powder</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baking_soda" title="Baking soda">baking soda</a> release carbon dioxide when heated or if exposed to <a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">acids</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Beverages">Beverages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Beverages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Carbon dioxide is used to produce <a href="/wiki/Carbonation" title="Carbonation">carbonated</a> <a href="/wiki/Soft_drink" title="Soft drink">soft drinks</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soda_water" title="Soda water">soda water</a>. Traditionally, the carbonation of beer and sparkling wine came about through natural fermentation, but many manufacturers carbonate these drinks with carbon dioxide recovered from the fermentation process. In the case of bottled and kegged beer, the most common method used is carbonation with recycled carbon dioxide. With the exception of British <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Real_Ale" title="Real Ale">Real Ale</a>, draught beer is usually transferred from kegs in a cold room or cellar to dispensing taps on the bar using pressurized carbon dioxide, sometimes mixed with nitrogen.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Wine_making">Wine making</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Wine making">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dry_ice_used_to_preserve_grapes_after_harvest.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3072" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Dry_ice_used_to_preserve_grapes_after_harvest.jpg/220px-Dry_ice_used_to_preserve_grapes_after_harvest.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Dry_ice_used_to_preserve_grapes_after_harvest.jpg/330px-Dry_ice_used_to_preserve_grapes_after_harvest.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Dry_ice_used_to_preserve_grapes_after_harvest.jpg/440px-Dry_ice_used_to_preserve_grapes_after_harvest.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dry_ice_used_to_preserve_grapes_after_harvest.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dry ice used to preserve grapes after harvest.</div></div></div>
<p>Carbon dioxide in the form of <a href="/wiki/Dry_ice" title="Dry ice">dry ice</a> is often used during the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Cold_soak&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Cold soak (page does not exist)">cold soak</a> phase in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wine_making" title="Wine making">wine making</a> to cool clusters of <a href="/wiki/Grape" title="Grape">grapes</a> quickly after picking to help prevent spontaneous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fermentation_(wine)" title="Fermentation (wine)">fermentation</a> by wild <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yeast_(wine)" title="Yeast (wine)">yeast</a>. The main advantage of using dry ice over water ice is that it cools the grapes without adding any additional water that might decrease the <a href="/wiki/Sugar" title="Sugar">sugar</a> concentration in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grape_must" title="Grape must">grape must</a>, and thus the <a href="/wiki/Alcohol" title="Alcohol">alcohol</a> concentration in the finished wine. Carbon dioxide is also used to create a hypoxic environment for <a href="/wiki/Carbonic_maceration" title="Carbonic maceration">carbonic maceration</a>, the process used to produce <a href="/wiki/Beaujolais" title="Beaujolais">Beaujolais</a> wine.
</p><p>Carbon dioxide is sometimes used to top up wine bottles or other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Storage_(wine)" title="Storage (wine)">storage</a> vessels such as barrels to prevent oxidation, though it has the problem that it can dissolve into the wine, making a previously still wine slightly fizzy. For this reason, other gases such as <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> or <a href="/wiki/Argon" title="Argon">argon</a> are preferred for this process by professional wine makers.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Stunning_animals">Stunning animals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Stunning animals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Carbon dioxide is often used to "stun" animals before slaughter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> "Stunning" may be a misnomer, as the animals are not knocked out immediately and may suffer distress.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Campbell_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Campbell-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inert_gas">Inert gas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Inert gas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>It is one of the most commonly used compressed gases for pneumatic (pressurized gas) systems in portable pressure tools. Carbon dioxide is also used as an atmosphere for <a href="/wiki/Welding" title="Welding">welding</a>, although in the welding arc, it reacts to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxidation" title="Oxidation">oxidize</a> most metals. Use in the automotive industry is common despite significant evidence that welds made in carbon dioxide are more <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brittle" title="Brittle">brittle</a> than those made in more inert atmospheres. It is used as a welding gas primarily because it is much less expensive than more inert gases such as <a href="/wiki/Argon" title="Argon">argon</a> or <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="why not use nitrogen? (July 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>  When used for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MIG_welding" title="MIG welding">MIG welding</a>, CO<sub>2</sub> use is sometimes referred to as MAG welding, for Metal Active Gas, as CO<sub>2</sub> can react at these high temperatures. It tends to produce a hotter puddle than truly inert atmospheres, improving the flow characteristics. Although, this may be due to atmospheric reactions occurring at the puddle site. This is usually the opposite of the desired effect when welding, as it tends to embrittle the site, but may not be a problem for general mild steel welding, where ultimate ductility is not a major concern.
</p><p>It is used in many consumer products that require pressurized gas because it is inexpensive and nonflammable, and because it undergoes a phase transition from gas to liquid at room temperature at an attainable pressure of approximately 60 <a href="/wiki/Bar_(unit)" title="Bar (unit)">bar</a> (870 psi, 59 atm), allowing far more carbon dioxide to fit in a given container than otherwise would. Life jackets often contain canisters of pressured carbon dioxide for quick inflation. <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">Aluminium</a> capsules of CO<sub>2</sub> are also sold as supplies of compressed gas for <a href="/wiki/Air_gun" title="Air gun">air guns</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paintball" title="Paintball">paintball</a> markers/guns, inflating bicycle tires, and for making <a href="/wiki/Carbonated_water" title="Carbonated water">carbonated water</a>. Rapid vaporization of liquid carbon dioxide is used for blasting in coal mines.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> High concentrations of carbon dioxide can also be used to kill pests. Liquid carbon dioxide is used in <a href="/wiki/Supercritical_drying" title="Supercritical drying">supercritical drying</a> of some food products and technological materials, in the preparation of specimens for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scanning_electron_microscopy" title="Scanning electron microscopy">scanning electron microscopy</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> and in the <a href="/wiki/Decaffeination" title="Decaffeination">decaffeination</a> of <a href="/wiki/Coffee" title="Coffee">coffee</a> beans.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fire_extinguisher">Fire extinguisher</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Fire extinguisher">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:US_Army_53023_Fire_Prevention_Week.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2848" data-file-width="4288" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/US_Army_53023_Fire_Prevention_Week.jpg/220px-US_Army_53023_Fire_Prevention_Week.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/US_Army_53023_Fire_Prevention_Week.jpg/330px-US_Army_53023_Fire_Prevention_Week.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/US_Army_53023_Fire_Prevention_Week.jpg/440px-US_Army_53023_Fire_Prevention_Week.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:US_Army_53023_Fire_Prevention_Week.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Use of a CO<sub>2</sub> fire extinguisher.</div></div></div>
<p>Carbon dioxide can be used to extinguish flames by flooding the environment around the flame with the gas. It does not itself react to extinguish the flame, but starves the flame of oxygen by displacing it. Some <a href="/wiki/Fire_extinguisher#Clean_agents_and_carbon_dioxide" title="Fire extinguisher">fire extinguishers</a>, especially those designed for electrical fires, contain liquid carbon dioxide under pressure. Carbon dioxide extinguishers work well on small flammable liquid and electrical fires, but not on ordinary combustible fires, because although it excludes oxygen, it does not cool the burning substances significantly and when the carbon dioxide disperses they are free to catch fire upon exposure to atmospheric oxygen. Their desirability in electrical fire stems from the fact that, unlike water or other chemical based methods, Carbon dioxide will not cause short circuits, leading to even more damage to equipment. Because it is a gas, it is also easy to dispense large amounts of the gas automatically in IT infrastructure rooms, where the fire itself might be hard to reach with more immediate methods because it is behind rack doors and inside of cases. Carbon dioxide has also been widely used as an extinguishing agent in fixed fire protection systems for local application of specific hazards and total flooding of a protected space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/International_Maritime_Organization" title="International Maritime Organization">International Maritime Organization</a> standards also recognize carbon dioxide systems for fire protection of ship holds and engine rooms. Carbon dioxide based fire protection systems have been linked to several deaths, because it can cause suffocation in sufficiently high concentrations. A review of CO<sub>2</sub> systems identified 51 incidents between 1975 and the date of the report (2000), causing 72 deaths and 145 injuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Supercritical_CO2_as_solvent">Supercritical CO<sub>2</sub> as solvent</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Supercritical CO2 as solvent">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Supercritical_carbon_dioxide" title="Supercritical carbon dioxide">Supercritical carbon dioxide</a></div>
<p>Liquid carbon dioxide is a good <a href="/wiki/Solvent" title="Solvent">solvent</a> for many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lipophilic" title="Lipophilic">lipophilic</a> <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic compounds</a> and is used to remove <a href="/wiki/Caffeine" title="Caffeine">caffeine</a> from <a href="/wiki/Coffee" title="Coffee">coffee</a>. Carbon dioxide has attracted attention in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pharmaceutical" title="Pharmaceutical">pharmaceutical</a> and other chemical processing industries as a less toxic alternative to more traditional solvents such as <a href="/wiki/Organochloride" title="Organochloride">organochlorides</a>. It is used by some <a href="/wiki/Dry_cleaning" title="Dry cleaning">dry cleaners</a> for this reason (see <a href="/wiki/Green_chemistry" title="Green chemistry">green chemistry</a>). It is used in the preparation of some <a href="/wiki/Aerogel#Production" title="Aerogel">aerogels</a> because of the properties of supercritical carbon dioxide.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Agricultural_and_biological_applications">Agricultural and biological applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Agricultural and biological applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Plants require carbon dioxide to conduct <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a>. The atmospheres of greenhouses may (if of large size, must) be enriched with additional CO<sub>2</sub> to sustain and increase the rate of plant growth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> At very high concentrations (100 times atmospheric concentration, or greater), carbon dioxide can be toxic to animal life, so raising the concentration to 10,000 ppm (1%) or higher for several hours will eliminate pests such as <a href="/wiki/Whitefly" title="Whitefly">whiteflies</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spider_mite" title="Spider mite">spider mites</a> in a greenhouse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>It has been proposed that CO<sub>2</sub> from power generation be bubbled into ponds to stimulate growth of algae that could then be converted into <a href="/wiki/Biodiesel" title="Biodiesel">biodiesel</a> fuel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-csmon_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-csmon-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medical_and_pharmacological_uses">Medical and pharmacological uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Medical and pharmacological uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In medicine, up to 5% carbon dioxide (130 times atmospheric concentration) is added to <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> for stimulation of breathing after <a href="/wiki/Apnea" title="Apnea">apnea</a> and to stabilize the <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>/CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> balance in blood.
</p><p>Carbon dioxide can be mixed with up to 50% oxygen, forming an inhalable gas; this is known as <a href="/wiki/Carbogen" title="Carbogen">Carbogen</a> and has a variety of medical and research uses.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oil_recovery">Oil recovery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Oil recovery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Carbon dioxide is used in <a href="/wiki/Enhanced_oil_recovery" title="Enhanced oil recovery">enhanced oil recovery</a> where it is injected into or adjacent to producing oil wells, usually under <a href="/wiki/Supercritical_fluid" title="Supercritical fluid">supercritical</a> conditions, when it becomes miscible with the oil. This approach can increase original oil recovery by reducing residual oil saturation by between 7% to 23% additional to <a href="/wiki/Extraction_of_petroleum#Primary_recovery" title="Extraction of petroleum">primary extraction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> It acts as both a pressurizing agent and, when dissolved into the underground <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crude_oil" title="Crude oil">crude oil</a>, significantly reduces its viscosity, and changing surface chemistry enabling the oil to flow more rapidly through the reservoir to the removal well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> In mature oil fields, extensive pipe networks are used to carry the carbon dioxide to the injection points.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bio_transformation_into_fuel">Bio transformation into fuel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Bio transformation into fuel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_capture_and_utilization" title="Carbon capture and utilization">Carbon capture and utilization</a></div>
<p>A strain of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cyanobacterium" title="Cyanobacterium">cyanobacterium</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Synechococcus" title="Synechococcus">Synechococcus elongatus</a></i> has been genetically engineered to produce the fuels <a href="/wiki/Isobutyraldehyde" title="Isobutyraldehyde">isobutyraldehyde</a> and <a href="/wiki/Isobutanol" title="Isobutanol">isobutanol</a> from CO<sub>2</sub> using photosynthesis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Refrigerant">Refrigerant</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Refrigerant">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Comparison_carbon_dioxide_water_phase_diagrams.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="235" data-file-width="512" height="202" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Comparison_carbon_dioxide_water_phase_diagrams.svg/440px-Comparison_carbon_dioxide_water_phase_diagrams.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Comparison_carbon_dioxide_water_phase_diagrams.svg/660px-Comparison_carbon_dioxide_water_phase_diagrams.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Comparison_carbon_dioxide_water_phase_diagrams.svg/880px-Comparison_carbon_dioxide_water_phase_diagrams.svg.png 2x" width="440"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Comparison_carbon_dioxide_water_phase_diagrams.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Comparison of phase diagrams of carbon dioxide (red) and water (blue) as a log-lin chart with phase transitions points at 1 atmosphere</div></div></div>
